<p><b>Metabolism</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>, from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">μεταβολή</span> <i>metabolē</i>, "change") is the set of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>-sustaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_reactions" title="Chemical reactions">chemical reactions</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisms" title="Organisms">organisms</a>. The three main purposes of metabolism are: the conversion of the energy in food to <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> available to run cellular processes; the conversion of food to building blocks for <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>; and the elimination of <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste" title="Metabolic waste">metabolic wastes</a>. These <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>-catalyzed reactions allow organisms to grow and reproduce, maintain their structures, and respond to their environments. The word metabolism can also refer to the sum of all chemical reactions that occur in living organisms, including <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a> and the transportation of substances into and between different cells, in which case the above described set of reactions within the cells is called intermediary (or intermediate) metabolism. 
</p><p>Metabolic reactions may be categorized as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catabolic" title="Catabolic">catabolic</a></i> – the <i>breaking down</i> of compounds (for example, of glucose to pyruvate by <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>); or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabolic" title="Anabolic">anabolic</a></i> – the <i>building up</i> (<a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">synthesis</a>) of compounds (such as proteins, carbohydrates, lipids, and nucleic acids). Usually, catabolism releases energy, and anabolism consumes energy.
</p><p>The chemical reactions of metabolism are organized into <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a>, in which one chemical is transformed through a series of steps into another chemical, each step being facilitated by a specific <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>. Enzymes are crucial to metabolism because they allow organisms to drive desirable reactions that require <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and will not occur by themselves, by <a href="/wiki/Coupling_(physics)" title="Coupling (physics)">coupling</a> them to <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_process" title="Spontaneous process">spontaneous reactions</a> that release energy. Enzymes act as <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysts</a> – they allow a reaction to proceed more rapidly – and they also allow the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway#Regulation" title="Metabolic pathway">regulation</a> of the rate of a  metabolic reaction, for example in response to changes in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell's</a> environment or to <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">signals</a> from other cells.
</p><p>The metabolic system of a particular organism determines which substances it will find <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutritious</a> and which <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisonous</a>. For example, some <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> use <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> as a nutrient, yet this gas is poisonous to animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physiology1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physiology1-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">basal metabolic rate</a> of an organism is the measure of the amount of energy consumed by all of these chemical reactions.
</p><p>A striking feature of metabolism is the similarity of the basic metabolic pathways among vastly different species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> For example, the set of <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acids</a> that are best known as the intermediates in the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a> are present in all known organisms, being found in species as diverse as the <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">unicellular</a> bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> and huge <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a> like <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithE_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-SmithE-3">[3]</a></sup> These similarities in metabolic pathways are likely due to their early appearance in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">evolutionary history</a>, and their retention is likely due to their <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">efficacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebenhoh_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebenhoh-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cascante_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cascante-5">[5]</a></sup> In various diseases, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_diabetes" title="Type II diabetes">type II diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, normal metabolism is disrupted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2018_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2018-6">[6]</a></sup> The metabolism of cancer cells is also different from the metabolism of normal cells, and these differences can be used to find targets for therapeutic intervention in cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vander_Heiden_2017_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vander_Heiden_2017-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_biochemicals">Key biochemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Key biochemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">Biomolecule</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">Cell (biology)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">Biochemistry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="893" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png/250px-Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png/375px-Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png/500px-Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triacylglycerol" title="Triacylglycerol">triacylglycerol</a> lipid</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1792" data-file-width="2054" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg/220px-Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg/330px-Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg/440px-Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This is a diagram depicting a large set of human metabolic pathways.</div></div></div>
<p>Most of the structures that make up animals, plants and microbes are made from four basic classes of <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>: <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> (often called <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>). As these molecules are vital for life, metabolic reactions either focus on making these molecules during the construction of cells and tissues, or on breaking them down and using them to obtain energy, by their digestion. These biochemicals can be joined to make <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, essential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macromolecules" title="Macromolecules">macromolecules</a> of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;">

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amino_acids_and_proteins">Amino acids and proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Amino acids and proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">Protein</a></div>
<p>Proteins are made of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> arranged in a linear chain joined by <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bonds</a>. Many proteins are <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyze</a> the chemical reactions in metabolism. Other proteins have structural or mechanical functions, such as those that form the <a href="/wiki/Cytoskeleton" title="Cytoskeleton">cytoskeleton</a>, a system of <a href="/wiki/Scaffolding" title="Scaffolding">scaffolding</a> that maintains the cell shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Proteins are also important in <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">cell signaling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">immune responses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cell_adhesion" title="Cell adhesion">cell adhesion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">active transport</a> across membranes, and the <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-10">[10]</a></sup> Amino acids also contribute to cellular energy metabolism by providing a carbon source for entry into the citric acid cycle (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tricarboxylic_acid_cycle" title="Tricarboxylic acid cycle">tricarboxylic acid cycle</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> especially when a primary source of energy, such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, is scarce, or when cells undergo metabolic stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lipids">Lipids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lipids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biolipid" title="Biolipid">Biolipid</a></div>
<p>Lipids are the most diverse group of biochemicals. Their main structural uses are as part of <a href="/wiki/Biological_membrane" title="Biological membrane">biological membranes</a> both internal and external, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-10">[10]</a></sup> Their <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">chemical energy</a> can also be used. Lipids are the polymers of fatty acids<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that contain a long, non-polar hydrocarbon chain with a small polar region containing oxygen. Lipids are usually defined as <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphiphiles" title="Amphiphiles">amphipathic</a> biological molecules but will dissolve in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_solvent" title="Organic solvent">organic solvents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> are a large group of compounds that contain <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>; a glycerol molecule attached to three fatty acids by <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester</a> linkages is called a <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triacylglyceride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Several variations on this basic structure exist, including backbones such as <a href="/wiki/Sphingosine" title="Sphingosine">sphingosine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sphingomyelin" title="Sphingomyelin">sphingomyelin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrophile" title="Hydrophile">hydrophilic</a> groups such as <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> as in <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>. <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">Steroids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sterol" title="Sterol">sterol</a> are another major class of lipids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates">Carbohydrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:233px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif"><img alt="The straight chain form consists of four C H O H groups linked in a row, capped at the ends by an aldehyde group C O H and a methanol group C H 2 O H. To form the ring, the aldehyde group combines with the O H group of the next-to-last carbon at the other end, just before the methanol group." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="181" data-file-width="231" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif" width="231"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">Glucose</a> can exist in both a straight-chain and ring form.</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">Carbohydrate</a></div>
<p>Carbohydrates are <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>, with many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> groups attached, that can exist as straight chains or rings. Carbohydrates are the most abundant biological molecules, and fill numerous roles, such as the storage and transport of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>) and structural components (<a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> in plants, <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> in animals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-10">[10]</a></sup> The basic carbohydrate units are called <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> and include <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>, and most importantly <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>. Monosaccharides can be linked together to form <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> in almost limitless ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleotides">Nucleotides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nucleotides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">Nucleotide</a></div>
<p>The two nucleic acids, DNA and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, are polymers of <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>. Each nucleotide is composed of a phosphate attached to a <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a> or <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a> sugar group which is attached to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogenous_base" title="Nitrogenous base">nitrogenous base</a>. Nucleic acids are critical for the storage and use of genetic information, and its interpretation through the processes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein biosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-10">[10]</a></sup> This information is protected by <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a> mechanisms and propagated through <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> have an <a href="/wiki/RNA_virus" title="RNA virus">RNA genome</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>, which uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_transcription" title="Reverse transcription">reverse transcription</a> to create a DNA template from its viral RNA genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> RNA in <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozymes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Spliceosome" title="Spliceosome">spliceosomes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> is similar to enzymes as it can catalyze chemical reactions. Individual <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside" title="Nucleoside">nucleosides</a> are made by attaching a <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobase</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a> sugar. These bases are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterocyclic" title="Heterocyclic">heterocyclic</a> rings containing nitrogen, classified as <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">purines</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidines</a>. Nucleotides also act as coenzymes in metabolic-group-transfer reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wimmer_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wimmer-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coenzymes">Coenzymes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Coenzymes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="187" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg/300px-Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg/450px-Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg/600px-Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">coenzyme</a> <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a>.The transferable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetyl" title="Acetyl">acetyl group</a> is bonded to the sulfur atom at the extreme left.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">Coenzyme</a></div>
<p>Metabolism involves a vast array of chemical reactions, but most fall under a few basic types of reactions that involve the transfer of <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional groups</a> of atoms and their bonds within molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> This common chemistry allows cells to use a small set of metabolic intermediates to carry chemical groups between different reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wimmer_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wimmer-18">[18]</a></sup> These group-transfer intermediates are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">coenzymes</a>. Each class of group-transfer reactions is carried out by a particular coenzyme, which is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substrate_(biochemistry)" title="Substrate (biochemistry)">substrate</a> for a set of enzymes that produce it, and a set of enzymes that consume it. These coenzymes are therefore continuously made, consumed and then recycled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimroth_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dimroth-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>One central coenzyme is <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the universal energy currency of cells. This nucleotide is used to transfer chemical energy between different chemical reactions. There is only a small amount of ATP in cells, but as it is continuously regenerated, the human body can use about its own weight in ATP per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimroth_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dimroth-20">[20]</a></sup> ATP acts as a bridge between <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>. Catabolism breaks down molecules, and anabolism puts them together. Catabolic reactions generate ATP, and anabolic reactions consume it. It also serves as a carrier of phosphate groups in <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylation</a> reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamin</a> is an organic compound needed in small quantities that cannot be made in cells. In <a href="/wiki/Human_nutrition" title="Human nutrition">human nutrition</a>, most vitamins function as coenzymes after modification; for example, all water-soluble vitamins are phosphorylated or are coupled to nucleotides when they are used in cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide">Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide</a> (NAD<sup>+</sup>), a derivative of vitamin B<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Niacin_(nutrient)" title="Niacin (nutrient)">niacin</a>), is an important coenzyme that acts as a hydrogen acceptor. Hundreds of separate types of <a href="/wiki/Dehydrogenase" title="Dehydrogenase">dehydrogenases</a> remove electrons from their substrates and <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduce</a> NAD<sup>+</sup> into NADH. This reduced form of the coenzyme is then a substrate for any of the <a href="/wiki/Reductase" title="Reductase">reductases</a> in the cell that need to transfer hydrogen atoms to their substrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide exists in two related forms in the cell, NADH and NADPH. The NAD<sup>+</sup>/NADH form is more important in catabolic reactions, while NADP<sup>+</sup>/NADPH is used in anabolic reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/300px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/450px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/600px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The structure of iron-containing <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>. The protein subunits are in red and blue, and the iron-containing <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> groups in green. From <span class="plainlinks"><a href="/wiki/Protein_Data_Bank" title="Protein Data Bank">PDB</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.rcsb.org/structure/1GZX" rel="nofollow">1GZX</a></span>​.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mineral_and_cofactors">Mineral and cofactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mineral and cofactors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bioinorganic_chemistry" title="Bioinorganic chemistry">Bioinorganic chemistry</a></div>
<p>Inorganic elements play critical roles in metabolism; some are abundant (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>) while others function at minute concentrations. About 99% of a human's body weight is made up of the elements <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>. <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">Organic compounds</a> (proteins, lipids and carbohydrates) contain the majority of the carbon and nitrogen; most of the oxygen and hydrogen is present as water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heymsfield_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heymsfield-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The abundant inorganic elements act as <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a>. The most important ions are <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> and the organic ion <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a>. The maintenance of precise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_gradient" title="Ion gradient">ion gradients</a> across <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a> maintains <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_pressure" title="Osmotic pressure">osmotic pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Ions are also critical for <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerve</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> function, as <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potentials</a> in these tissues are produced by the exchange of electrolytes between the <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_fluid" title="Extracellular fluid">extracellular fluid</a> and the cell's fluid, the <a href="/wiki/Cytosol" title="Cytosol">cytosol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Electrolytes enter and leave cells through proteins in the cell membrane called <a href="/wiki/Ion_channel" title="Ion channel">ion channels</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">muscle contraction</a> depends upon the movement of calcium, sodium and potassium through ion channels in the cell membrane and <a href="/wiki/T-tubule" title="T-tubule">T-tubules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">Transition metals</a> are usually present as <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace elements</a> in organisms, with <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> being most abundant of those.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Metal cofactors are bound tightly to specific sites in proteins; although enzyme cofactors can be modified during catalysis, they always return to their original state by the end of the reaction catalyzed. Metal micronutrients are taken up into organisms by specific transporters and bind to storage proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Ferritin" title="Ferritin">ferritin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Metallothionein" title="Metallothionein">metallothionein</a> when not in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Catabolism">Catabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Catabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">Catabolism</a></div>
<p>Catabolism is the set of metabolic processes that break down large molecules. These include breaking down and oxidizing food molecules. The purpose of the catabolic reactions is to provide the energy and components needed by anabolic reactions which build molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_2002_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts_2002-32">[32]</a></sup> The exact nature of these catabolic reactions differ from organism to organism, and organisms can be classified based on their sources of energy, hydrogen, and carbon (their <a href="/wiki/Primary_nutritional_groups" title="Primary nutritional groups">primary nutritional groups</a>), as shown in the table below. Organic molecules are used as a source of hydrogen atoms or electrons by <a href="/wiki/Organotroph" title="Organotroph">organotrophs</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Lithotroph" title="Lithotroph">lithotrophs</a> use inorganic substrates. Whereas <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">phototrophs</a> convert sunlight to <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy#Chemical_potential_energy" title="Potential energy">chemical energy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chemotroph" title="Chemotroph">chemotrophs</a> depend on <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reactions that involve the transfer of electrons from reduced donor molecules such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ferrous" title="Ferrous">ferrous ions</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a>. In animals, these reactions involve complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a> that are broken down to simpler molecules, such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water. <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">Photosynthetic</a> organisms, such as plants and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, use similar electron-transfer reactions to store energy absorbed from sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson2004_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson2004-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable float-right" style="text-align:center; width:50%;">

<p>The most common set of catabolic reactions in animals can be separated into three main stages. In the first stage, large organic molecules, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, are digested into their smaller components outside cells. Next, these smaller molecules are taken up by cells and converted to smaller molecules, usually <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl coenzyme A</a> (acetyl-CoA), which releases some energy. Finally, the acetyl group on acetyl-CoA is oxidized to water and carbon dioxide in the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport chain</a>, releasing more energy while reducing the coenzyme <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide">nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide</a> (NAD<sup>+</sup>) into NADH.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_2002_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts_2002-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestion">Digestion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Digestion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">Digestion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">Gastrointestinal tract</a></div>
<p>Macromolecules cannot be directly processed by cells. Macromolecules must be broken into smaller units before they can be used in cell metabolism. Different classes of enzymes are used to digest these polymers. These <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">digestive enzymes</a> include <a href="/wiki/Protease" title="Protease">proteases</a> that digest proteins into amino acids, as well as <a href="/wiki/Glycoside_hydrolase" title="Glycoside hydrolase">glycoside hydrolases</a> that digest polysaccharides into simple sugars known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monosaccharides" title="Monosaccharides">monosaccharides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Microbes simply secrete digestive enzymes into their surroundings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> while animals only secrete these enzymes from specialized cells in their <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">guts</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Salivary_gland" title="Salivary gland">salivary glands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The amino acids or sugars released by these extracellular enzymes are then pumped into cells by <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">active transport</a> proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Catabolism_schematic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/300px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/450px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/600px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Catabolism_schematic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simplified outline of the catabolism of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_from_organic_compounds">Energy from organic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Energy from organic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">Cellular respiration</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">Fermentation (biochemistry)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate_catabolism" title="Carbohydrate catabolism">Carbohydrate catabolism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fat_catabolism" title="Fat catabolism">Fat catabolism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protein_catabolism" title="Protein catabolism">Protein catabolism</a></div>
<p>Carbohydrate catabolism is the breakdown of carbohydrates into smaller units. Carbohydrates are usually taken into cells after they have been digested into <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Once inside, the major route of breakdown is <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a>, where sugars such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> are converted into <a href="/wiki/Pyruvic_acid" title="Pyruvic acid">pyruvate</a> and some ATP is generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouche_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bouche-43">[43]</a></sup> Pyruvate is an intermediate in several metabolic pathways, but the majority is converted to <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a> through aerobic (with oxygen) glycolysis and fed into the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>. Although some more ATP is generated in the citric acid cycle, the most important product is NADH, which is made from NAD<sup>+</sup> as the acetyl-CoA is oxidized. This oxidation releases <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> as a waste product. In anaerobic conditions, glycolysis produces <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a>, through the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Lactate_dehydrogenase" title="Lactate dehydrogenase">lactate dehydrogenase</a> re-oxidizing NADH to NAD+ for re-use in glycolysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> An alternative route for glucose breakdown is the <a href="/wiki/Pentose_phosphate_pathway" title="Pentose phosphate pathway">pentose phosphate pathway</a>, which reduces the coenzyme <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADPH" title="NADPH">NADPH</a> and produces <a href="/wiki/Pentose" title="Pentose">pentose</a> sugars such as <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a>, the sugar component of <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.
</p><p>Fats are catabolized by <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> to free fatty acids and glycerol. The glycerol enters glycolysis and the fatty acids are broken down by <a href="/wiki/Beta_oxidation" title="Beta oxidation">beta oxidation</a> to release acetyl-CoA, which then is fed into the citric acid cycle. Fatty acids release more energy upon oxidation than carbohydrates. Steroids are also broken down by some bacteria in a process similar to beta oxidation, and this breakdown process involves the release of significant amounts of acetyl-CoA, propionyl-CoA, and pyruvate, which can all be used by the cell for energy. <i>M. tuberculosis</i> can also grow on the lipid <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> as a sole source of carbon, and genes involved in the cholesterol-use pathway(s) have been validated as important during various stages of the infection lifecycle of <i>M. tuberculosis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">Amino acids</a> are either used to synthesize proteins and other biomolecules, or oxidized to <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and carbon dioxide to produce energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The oxidation pathway starts with the removal of the amino group by a <a href="/wiki/Transaminase" title="Transaminase">transaminase</a>. The amino group is fed into the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a>, leaving a deaminated carbon skeleton in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Keto_acid" title="Keto acid">keto acid</a>. Several of these keto acids are intermediates in the citric acid cycle, for example α-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-Ketoglutaric_acid" title="Alpha-Ketoglutaric acid">ketoglutarate</a> formed by deamination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Glucogenic_amino_acid" title="Glucogenic amino acid">glucogenic amino acids</a> can also be converted into glucose, through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a> (discussed below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_transformations">Energy transformations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Energy transformations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidative_phosphorylation">Oxidative phosphorylation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Oxidative phosphorylation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">Oxidative phosphorylation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">Chemiosmosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">Mitochondrion</a></div>
<p>In oxidative phosphorylation, the electrons removed from organic molecules in areas such as the citric acid cycle are transferred to oxygen and the energy released is used to make ATP. This is done in <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> by a series of proteins in the membranes of mitochondria called the <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport chain</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a>, these proteins are found in the cell's <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_cell_structure" title="Bacterial cell structure">inner membrane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> These proteins use the energy from <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reduced</a> molecules like NADH to pump <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> across a membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ATPsyn.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/ATPsyn.gif/220px-ATPsyn.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/ATPsyn.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ATPsyn.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mechanism of <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a>. ATP is shown in red, ADP and phosphate in pink and the rotating stalk subunit in black.</div></div></div>
<p>Pumping protons out of the mitochondria creates a proton <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">concentration difference</a> across the membrane and generates an <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">electrochemical gradient</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> This force drives protons back into the mitochondrion through the base of an enzyme called <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a>. The flow of protons makes the stalk subunit rotate, causing the <a href="/wiki/Active_site" title="Active site">active site</a> of the synthase domain to change shape and phosphorylate <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_diphosphate" title="Adenosine diphosphate">adenosine diphosphate</a> – turning it into ATP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimroth_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dimroth-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_from_inorganic_compounds">Energy from inorganic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Energy from inorganic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Microbial_metabolism" title="Microbial metabolism">Microbial metabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">Nitrogen cycle</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemolithotroph" title="Chemolithotroph">Chemolithotrophy</a> is a type of metabolism found in <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> where energy is obtained from the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compounds</a>. These organisms can use <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> reduced <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> compounds (such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thiosulfate" title="Thiosulfate">thiosulfate</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physiology1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Physiology1-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_oxide" title="Iron(II) oxide">ferrous iron (Fe(II))</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> as sources of reducing power and they gain energy from the oxidation of these compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> These microbial processes are important in global <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycles</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Acetogenesis" title="Acetogenesis">acetogenesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrification" title="Nitrification">nitrification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denitrification" title="Denitrification">denitrification</a> and are critical for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_(soil)" title="Fertility (soil)">soil fertility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_from_light">Energy from light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Energy from light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">Phototroph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photophosphorylation" title="Photophosphorylation">Photophosphorylation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">Chloroplast</a></div>
<p>The energy in sunlight is captured by <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purple_bacteria" title="Purple bacteria">purple bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_sulfur_bacteria" title="Green sulfur bacteria">green sulfur bacteria</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>. This process is often coupled to the conversion of carbon dioxide into organic compounds, as part of photosynthesis, which is discussed below. The energy capture and carbon fixation systems can, however, operate separately in prokaryotes, as purple bacteria and green sulfur bacteria can use sunlight as a source of energy, while switching between carbon fixation and the fermentation of organic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many organisms, the capture of solar energy is similar in principle to oxidative phosphorylation, as it involves the storage of energy as a proton concentration gradient. This proton motive force then drives ATP synthesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The electrons needed to drive this electron transport chain come from light-gathering proteins called <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_reaction_centre" title="Photosynthetic reaction centre">photosynthetic reaction centres</a>. Reaction centers are classified into two types depending on the nature of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_pigment" title="Photosynthetic pigment">photosynthetic pigment</a> present, with most photosynthetic bacteria only having one type, while plants and cyanobacteria have two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In plants, algae, and cyanobacteria, <a href="/wiki/Photosystem" title="Photosystem">photosystem II</a> uses light energy to remove electrons from water, releasing oxygen as a waste product. The electrons then flow to the <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b6f_complex" title="Cytochrome b6f complex">cytochrome b6f complex</a>, which uses their energy to pump protons across the <a href="/wiki/Thylakoid" title="Thylakoid">thylakoid</a> membrane in the <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson2004_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson2004-34">[34]</a></sup> These protons move back through the membrane as they drive the ATP synthase, as before. The electrons then flow through <a href="/wiki/Photosystem" title="Photosystem">photosystem I</a> and can then be used to reduce the coenzyme NADP<sup>+</sup>.<sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></sup> This coenzyme can enter the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a>, which is discussed below, or be recycled for further ATP generation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anabolism">Anabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Anabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">Anabolism</a></div>
<p><b>Anabolism</b> is the set of constructive metabolic processes where the energy released by catabolism is used to synthesize complex molecules. In general, the complex molecules that make up cellular structures are constructed step-by-step from smaller and simpler precursors. Anabolism involves three basic stages. First, the production of precursors such as <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpenoid" title="Terpenoid">isoprenoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>, secondly, their activation into reactive forms using energy from ATP, and thirdly, the assembly of these precursors into complex molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anabolism in organisms can be different according to the source of constructed molecules in their cells. <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">Autotrophs</a> such as plants can construct the complex organic molecules in their cells such as polysaccharides and proteins from simple molecules like <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water. <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">Heterotrophs</a>, on the other hand, require a source of more complex substances, such as monosaccharides and amino acids, to produce these complex molecules. Organisms can be further classified by ultimate source of their energy: photoautotrophs and photoheterotrophs obtain energy from light, whereas chemoautotrophs and chemoheterotrophs obtain energy from oxidation reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_fixation">Carbon fixation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Carbon fixation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">Photosynthesis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">Carbon fixation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemosynthesis" title="Chemosynthesis">Chemosynthesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg/220px-Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg/330px-Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg/440px-Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plant cells (bounded by purple walls) filled with chloroplasts (green), which are the site of photosynthesis</div></div></div>
<p>Photosynthesis is the synthesis of carbohydrates from sunlight and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>). In plants, cyanobacteria and algae, oxygenic photosynthesis splits water, with oxygen produced as a waste product. This process uses the ATP and NADPH produced by the <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_reaction_centre" title="Photosynthetic reaction centre">photosynthetic reaction centres</a>, as described above, to convert CO<sub>2</sub> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerate_3-phosphate" title="Glycerate 3-phosphate">glycerate 3-phosphate</a>, which can then be converted into glucose. This carbon-fixation reaction is carried out by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/RuBisCO" title="RuBisCO">RuBisCO</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin – Benson cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Three types of photosynthesis occur in plants, <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">C3 carbon fixation</a>, <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 carbon fixation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crassulacean_acid_metabolism" title="Crassulacean acid metabolism">CAM photosynthesis</a>. These differ by the route that carbon dioxide takes to the Calvin cycle, with C3 plants fixing CO<sub>2</sub> directly, while C4 and CAM photosynthesis incorporate the CO<sub>2</sub> into other compounds first, as adaptations to deal with intense sunlight and dry conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In photosynthetic <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> the mechanisms of carbon fixation are more diverse. Here, carbon dioxide can be fixed by the Calvin – Benson cycle, a <a href="/wiki/Reverse_Krebs_cycle" title="Reverse Krebs cycle">reversed citric acid</a> cycle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> or the carboxylation of acetyl-CoA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Prokaryotic <a href="/wiki/Chemotroph" title="Chemotroph">chemoautotrophs</a> also fix CO<sub>2</sub> through the Calvin–Benson cycle, but use energy from inorganic compounds to drive the reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates_and_glycans">Carbohydrates and glycans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates and glycans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">Gluconeogenesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glyoxylate_cycle" title="Glyoxylate cycle">Glyoxylate cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogenesis" title="Glycogenesis">Glycogenesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Glycosylation" title="Glycosylation">Glycosylation</a></div>
<p>In carbohydrate anabolism, simple organic acids can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and then used to assemble <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>. The generation of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> from compounds like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerate_3-phosphate" title="Glycerate 3-phosphate">glycerate 3-phosphate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>. Gluconeogenesis converts pyruvate to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucose-6-phosphate" title="Glucose-6-phosphate">glucose-6-phosphate</a> through a series of intermediates, many of which are shared with <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouche_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bouche-43">[43]</a></sup> However, this pathway is not simply <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> run in reverse, as several steps are catalyzed by non-glycolytic enzymes. This is important as it allows the formation and breakdown of glucose to be regulated separately, and prevents both pathways from running simultaneously in a <a href="/wiki/Futile_cycle" title="Futile cycle">futile cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although fat is a common way of storing energy, in <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> such as humans the <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> in these stores cannot be converted to glucose through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a> as these organisms cannot convert acetyl-CoA into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a>; plants do, but animals do not, have the necessary enzymatic machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensign_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensign-72">[72]</a></sup> As a result, after long-term starvation, vertebrates need to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ketone_body" title="Ketone body">ketone bodies</a> from fatty acids to replace glucose in tissues such as the brain that cannot metabolize fatty acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In other organisms such as plants and bacteria, this metabolic problem is solved using the <a href="/wiki/Glyoxylate_cycle" title="Glyoxylate cycle">glyoxylate cycle</a>, which bypasses the <a href="/wiki/Decarboxylation" title="Decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a> step in the citric acid cycle and allows the transformation of acetyl-CoA to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxaloacetate" title="Oxaloacetate">oxaloacetate</a>, where it can be used for the production of glucose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensign_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensign-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kornberg_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kornberg-74">[74]</a></sup> Other than fat, glucose is stored in most tissues, as an energy resource available within the tissue through glycogenesis which was usually being used to maintained glucose level in blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polysaccharides and <a href="/wiki/Glycan" title="Glycan">glycans</a> are made by the sequential addition of monosaccharides by <a href="/wiki/Glycosyltransferase" title="Glycosyltransferase">glycosyltransferase</a> from a reactive sugar-phosphate donor such as <a href="/wiki/Uridine_diphosphate_glucose" title="Uridine diphosphate glucose">uridine diphosphate glucose</a> (UDP-Glc) to an acceptor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group on the growing polysaccharide. As any of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> groups on the ring of the substrate can be acceptors, the polysaccharides produced can have straight or branched structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The polysaccharides produced can have structural or metabolic functions themselves, or be transferred to lipids and proteins by enzymes called <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharyltransferase" title="Oligosaccharyltransferase">oligosaccharyltransferases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Fatty_acids.2C_isoprenoids_and_sterol"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fatty_acids,_isoprenoids_and_sterol">Fatty acids, isoprenoids and sterol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fatty acids, isoprenoids and sterol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_synthesis" title="Fatty acid synthesis">Fatty acid synthesis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_metabolism" title="Steroid metabolism">Steroid metabolism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sterol_synthesis.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="571" decoding="async" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sterol_synthesis.svg/350px-Sterol_synthesis.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sterol_synthesis.svg/525px-Sterol_synthesis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sterol_synthesis.svg/700px-Sterol_synthesis.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sterol_synthesis.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simplified version of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_synthesis" title="Steroid synthesis">steroid synthesis</a> pathway with the intermediates <a href="/wiki/Isopentenyl_pyrophosphate" title="Isopentenyl pyrophosphate">isopentenyl pyrophosphate</a> (IPP), <a href="/wiki/Dimethylallyl_pyrophosphate" title="Dimethylallyl pyrophosphate">dimethylallyl pyrophosphate</a> (DMAPP), <a href="/wiki/Geranyl_pyrophosphate" title="Geranyl pyrophosphate">geranyl pyrophosphate</a> (GPP) and <a href="/wiki/Squalene" title="Squalene">squalene</a> shown. Some intermediates are omitted for clarity.</div></div></div>
<p>Fatty acids are made by <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_synthase" title="Fatty acid synthase">fatty acid synthases</a> that polymerize and then reduce acetyl-CoA units. The acyl chains in the fatty acids are extended by a cycle of reactions that add the acyl group, reduce it to an alcohol, <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydrate</a> it to an <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkene</a> group and then reduce it again to an <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkane</a> group. The enzymes of fatty acid biosynthesis are divided into two groups: in animals and fungi, all these fatty acid synthase reactions are carried out by a single multifunctional type I protein,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> while in plant <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">plastids</a> and bacteria separate type II enzymes perform each step in the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">Terpenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terpenoid" title="Terpenoid">isoprenoids</a> are a large class of lipids that include the <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> and form the largest class of plant <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural products</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> These compounds are made by the assembly and modification of <a href="/wiki/Isoprene" title="Isoprene">isoprene</a> units donated from the reactive precursors <a href="/wiki/Isopentenyl_pyrophosphate" title="Isopentenyl pyrophosphate">isopentenyl pyrophosphate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dimethylallyl_pyrophosphate" title="Dimethylallyl pyrophosphate">dimethylallyl pyrophosphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuzuyama_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuzuyama-83">[83]</a></sup> These precursors can be made in different ways. In animals and archaea, the <a href="/wiki/Mevalonate_pathway" title="Mevalonate pathway">mevalonate pathway</a> produces these compounds from acetyl-CoA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> while in plants and bacteria the <a href="/wiki/Non-mevalonate_pathway" title="Non-mevalonate pathway">non-mevalonate pathway</a> uses pyruvate and <a href="/wiki/Glyceraldehyde_3-phosphate" title="Glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate">glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate</a> as substrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuzuyama_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuzuyama-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> One important reaction that uses these activated isoprene donors is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_biosynthesis" title="Steroid biosynthesis">sterol biosynthesis</a>. Here, the isoprene units are joined to make <a href="/wiki/Squalene" title="Squalene">squalene</a> and then folded up and formed into a set of rings to make <a href="/wiki/Lanosterol" title="Lanosterol">lanosterol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroepfer_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schroepfer-86">[86]</a></sup> Lanosterol can then be converted into other sterols such as <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ergosterol" title="Ergosterol">ergosterol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroepfer_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schroepfer-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proteins">Proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">Protein biosynthesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_synthesis" title="Amino acid synthesis">Amino acid synthesis</a></div>
<p>Organisms vary in their ability to synthesize the 20 common amino acids. Most bacteria and plants can synthesize all twenty, but mammals can only synthesize eleven nonessential amino acids, so nine <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a> must be obtained from food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-10">[10]</a></sup> Some simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>, such as the bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma_pneumoniae" title="Mycoplasma pneumoniae">Mycoplasma pneumoniae</a></i>, lack all amino acid synthesis and take their amino acids directly from their hosts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> All amino acids are synthesized from intermediates in glycolysis, the citric acid cycle, or the pentose phosphate pathway. Nitrogen is provided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a>. Nonessensial amino acid synthesis depends on the formation of the appropriate alpha-keto acid, which is then <a href="/wiki/Transaminase" title="Transaminase">transaminated</a> to form an amino acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Amino acids are made into proteins by being joined in a chain of <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bonds</a>. Each different protein has a unique sequence of amino acid residues: this is its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_structure" title="Primary structure">primary structure</a>. Just as the letters of the alphabet can be combined to form an almost endless variety of words, amino acids can be linked in varying sequences to form a huge variety of proteins. Proteins are made from amino acids that have been activated by attachment to a <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> molecule through an <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester</a> bond. This <a href="/wiki/Aminoacyl-tRNA" title="Aminoacyl-tRNA">aminoacyl-tRNA</a> precursor is produced in an <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>-dependent reaction carried out by an <a href="/wiki/Aminoacyl_tRNA_synthetase" title="Aminoacyl tRNA synthetase">aminoacyl tRNA synthetase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> This aminoacyl-tRNA is then a substrate for the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>, which joins the amino acid onto the elongating protein chain, using the sequence information in a <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleotide_synthesis_and_salvage">Nucleotide synthesis and salvage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nucleotide synthesis and salvage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide_salvage" title="Nucleotide salvage">Nucleotide salvage</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrimidine_biosynthesis" title="Pyrimidine biosynthesis">Pyrimidine biosynthesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Purine#Metabolism" title="Purine">Purine § Metabolism</a></div>
