<p>Nucleotides are made from amino acids, carbon dioxide and <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a> in pathways that require large amounts of metabolic energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudolph_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudolph-92">[92]</a></sup> Consequently, most organisms have efficient systems to salvage preformed nucleotides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudolph_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rudolph-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">Purines</a> are synthesized as <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside" title="Nucleoside">nucleosides</a> (bases attached to <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid_22531138_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid_22531138-94">[94]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> are made from the precursor nucleoside <a href="/wiki/Inosine" title="Inosine">inosine</a> monophosphate, which is synthesized using atoms from the amino acids <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aspartic_acid" title="Aspartic acid">aspartic acid</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Formate" title="Formate">formate</a> transferred from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">coenzyme</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folic_acid" title="Folic acid">tetrahydrofolate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">Pyrimidines</a>, on the other hand, are synthesized from the base <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrimidinecarboxylic_acid" title="Pyrimidinecarboxylic acid">orotate</a>, which is formed from glutamine and aspartate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Xenobiotics_and_redox_metabolism">Xenobiotics and redox metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Xenobiotics and redox metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenobiotic_metabolism" title="Xenobiotic metabolism">Xenobiotic metabolism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_metabolism" title="Drug metabolism">Drug metabolism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_metabolism" title="Alcohol metabolism">Alcohol metabolism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">Antioxidant</a></div>
<p>All organisms are constantly exposed to compounds that they cannot use as foods and that would be harmful if they accumulated in cells, as they have no metabolic function. These potentially damaging compounds are called <a href="/wiki/Xenobiotic" title="Xenobiotic">xenobiotics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Xenobiotics such as <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">synthetic drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">natural poisons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> are detoxified by a set of xenobiotic-metabolizing enzymes. In humans, these include <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_P450" title="Cytochrome P450">cytochrome P450 oxidases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glucuronosyltransferase" title="Glucuronosyltransferase">UDP-glucuronosyltransferases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_S-transferase" title="Glutathione S-transferase">glutathione <i>S</i>-transferases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> This system of enzymes acts in three stages to firstly oxidize the xenobiotic (phase I) and then conjugate water-soluble groups onto the molecule (phase II). The modified water-soluble xenobiotic can then be pumped out of cells and in multicellular organisms may be further metabolized before being excreted (phase III). In <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, these reactions are particularly important in microbial <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a> of pollutants and the <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a> of contaminated land and oil spills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Many of these microbial reactions are shared with multicellular organisms, but due to the incredible diversity of types of microbes these organisms are able to deal with a far wider range of xenobiotics than multicellular organisms, and can degrade even <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutants</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">organochloride</a> compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>A related problem for <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a> is <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-102">[102]</a></sup> Here, processes including <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a> and the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disulfide_bond" title="Disulfide bond">disulfide bonds</a> during <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">protein folding</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> These damaging oxidants are removed by <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidant</a> metabolites such as <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a> and enzymes such as <a href="/wiki/Catalase" title="Catalase">catalases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peroxidase" title="Peroxidase">peroxidases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sies_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sies-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vertuani_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vertuani-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamics_of_living_organisms">Thermodynamics of living organisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Thermodynamics of living organisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biological_thermodynamics" title="Biological thermodynamics">Biological thermodynamics</a></div>
<p>Living organisms must obey the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_thermodynamics" title="Laws of thermodynamics">laws of thermodynamics</a>, which describe the transfer of heat and <a href="/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)" title="Work (thermodynamics)">work</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> states that in any <a href="/wiki/Isolated_system" title="Isolated system">isolated system</a>, the amount of <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> (disorder) cannot decrease. Although living organisms' amazing complexity appears to contradict this law, life is possible as all organisms are <a href="/wiki/Open_system_(systems_theory)" title="Open system (systems theory)">open systems</a> that exchange matter and energy with their surroundings. Living systems are not in <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">equilibrium</a>, but instead are <a href="/wiki/Dissipative_system" title="Dissipative system">dissipative systems</a> that maintain their state of high complexity by causing a larger increase in the entropy of their environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The metabolism of a cell achieves this by coupling the <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_process" title="Spontaneous process">spontaneous processes</a> of catabolism to the non-spontaneous processes of anabolism. In <a href="/wiki/Non-equilibrium_thermodynamics" title="Non-equilibrium thermodynamics">thermodynamic</a> terms, metabolism maintains order by creating disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation_and_control">Regulation and control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Regulation and control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">Metabolic pathway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_control_analysis" title="Metabolic control analysis">Metabolic control analysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">Hormone</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulatory_enzymes" title="Regulatory enzymes">Regulatory enzymes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">Cell signaling</a></div>
<p>As the environments of most organisms are constantly changing, the reactions of metabolism must be finely <a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">regulated</a> to maintain a constant set of conditions within cells, a condition called <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Metabolic regulation also allows organisms to respond to signals and interact actively with their environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Two closely linked concepts are important for understanding how metabolic pathways are controlled. Firstly, the <i>regulation</i> of an enzyme in a pathway is how its activity is increased and decreased in response to signals. Secondly, the <i>control</i> exerted by this enzyme is the effect that these changes in its activity have on the overall rate of the pathway (the <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">flux</a> through the pathway).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salter_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salter-111">[111]</a></sup> For example, an enzyme may show large changes in activity (<i>i.e.</i> it is highly regulated) but if these changes have little effect on the flux of a metabolic pathway, then this enzyme is not involved in the control of the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="745" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg/300px-Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg/450px-Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg/600px-Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Effect of insulin on glucose uptake and metabolism.</b> Insulin binds to its receptor (1), which in turn starts many protein activation cascades (2). These include: translocation of Glut-4 transporter to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane" title="Plasma membrane">plasma membrane</a> and influx of glucose (3), <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> synthesis (4), <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> (5) and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acid</a> synthesis (6).</div></div></div>
<p>There are multiple levels of metabolic regulation. In intrinsic regulation, the metabolic pathway self-regulates to respond to changes in the levels of substrates or products; for example, a decrease in the amount of product can increase the <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">flux</a> through the pathway to compensate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salter_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Salter-111">[111]</a></sup> This type of regulation often involves <a href="/wiki/Allosteric_regulation" title="Allosteric regulation">allosteric regulation</a> of the activities of multiple enzymes in the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Extrinsic control involves a cell in a multicellular organism changing its metabolism in response to signals from other cells. These signals are usually in the form of water-soluble messengers such as <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Growth_factor" title="Growth factor">growth factors</a> and are detected by specific <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptors</a> on the cell surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> These signals are then transmitted inside the cell by <a href="/wiki/Second_messenger_system" title="Second messenger system">second messenger systems</a> that often involved the <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylation</a> of proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>A very well understood example of extrinsic control is the regulation of glucose metabolism by the hormone <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Insulin is produced in response to rises in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_sugar" title="Blood sugar">blood glucose levels</a>. Binding of the hormone to <a href="/wiki/Insulin_receptor" title="Insulin receptor">insulin receptors</a> on cells then activates a cascade of <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase" title="Protein kinase">protein kinases</a> that cause the cells to take up glucose and convert it into storage molecules such as fatty acids and <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The metabolism of glycogen is controlled by activity of <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylase" title="Phosphorylase">phosphorylase</a>, the enzyme that breaks down glycogen, and <a href="/wiki/Glycogen_synthase" title="Glycogen synthase">glycogen synthase</a>, the enzyme that makes it. These enzymes are regulated in a reciprocal fashion, with phosphorylation inhibiting glycogen synthase, but activating phosphorylase. Insulin causes glycogen synthesis by activating <a href="/wiki/Phosphatase" title="Phosphatase">protein phosphatases</a> and producing a decrease in the phosphorylation of these enzymes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_evolution" title="Molecular evolution">Molecular evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">Phylogenetics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_life_int.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tree_of_life_int.svg/400px-Tree_of_life_int.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tree_of_life_int.svg/600px-Tree_of_life_int.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tree_of_life_int.svg/800px-Tree_of_life_int.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_life_int.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Evolutionary tree</a> showing the common ancestry of organisms from all three <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domains</a> of life. <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> are colored blue, <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> red, and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> green. Relative positions of some of the <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> included are shown around the tree.</div></div></div>
<p>The central pathways of metabolism described above, such as glycolysis and the citric acid cycle, are present in all <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">three domains</a> of living things and were present in the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithE_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-SmithE-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> This universal ancestral cell was <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotic</a> and probably a <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogen</a> that had extensive amino acid, nucleotide, carbohydrate and lipid metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The retention of these ancient pathways during later <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> may be the result of these reactions having been an optimal solution to their particular metabolic problems, with pathways such as glycolysis and the citric acid cycle producing their end products highly efficiently and in a minimal number of steps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebenhoh_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ebenhoh-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cascante_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cascante-5">[5]</a></sup> The first pathways of enzyme-based metabolism may have been parts of <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">purine</a> nucleotide metabolism, while previous metabolic pathways were a part of the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_world_hypothesis" title="RNA world hypothesis">RNA world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many models have been proposed to describe the mechanisms by which novel metabolic pathways evolve. These include the sequential addition of novel enzymes to a short ancestral pathway, the duplication and then divergence of entire pathways as well as the recruitment of pre-existing enzymes and their assembly into a novel reaction pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The relative importance of these mechanisms is unclear, but genomic studies have shown that enzymes in a pathway are likely to have a shared ancestry, suggesting that many pathways have evolved in a step-by-step fashion with novel functions created from pre-existing steps in the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> An alternative model comes from studies that trace the evolution of proteins' structures in metabolic networks, this has suggested that enzymes are pervasively recruited, borrowing enzymes to perform similar functions in different metabolic pathways (evident in the <a href="/wiki/MANET_database" title="MANET database">MANET database</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> These recruitment processes result in an evolutionary enzymatic mosaic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> A third possibility is that some parts of metabolism might exist as "modules" that can be reused in different pathways and perform similar functions on different molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>As well as the evolution of new metabolic pathways, evolution can also cause the loss of metabolic functions. For example, in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> metabolic processes that are not essential for survival are lost and preformed amino acids, nucleotides and carbohydrates may instead be scavenged from the <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Similar reduced metabolic capabilities are seen in <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiotic</a> organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Investigation_and_manipulation">Investigation and manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Investigation and manipulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protein_methods" title="Protein methods">Protein methods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">Proteomics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolomics" title="Metabolomics">Metabolomics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_network_modelling" title="Metabolic network modelling">Metabolic network modelling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="836" decoding="async" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png/300px-A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png/450px-A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png/600px-A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Metabolic_network" title="Metabolic network">Metabolic network</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>. <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">Enzymes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolite" title="Metabolite">metabolites</a> are shown as red squares and the interactions between them as black lines.</div></div></div>
<p>Classically, metabolism is studied by a <a href="/wiki/Reductionism" title="Reductionism">reductionist</a> approach that focuses on a single metabolic pathway. Particularly valuable is the use of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracers</a> at the whole-organism, tissue and cellular levels, which define the paths from precursors to final products by identifying radioactively labelled intermediates and products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The enzymes that catalyze these chemical reactions can then be <a href="/wiki/Protein_purification" title="Protein purification">purified</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_kinetics" title="Enzyme kinetics">kinetics</a> and responses to <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_inhibitor" title="Enzyme inhibitor">inhibitors</a> investigated. A parallel approach is to identify the small molecules in a cell or tissue; the complete set of these molecules is called the <a href="/wiki/Metabolome" title="Metabolome">metabolome</a>. Overall, these studies give a good view of the structure and function of simple metabolic pathways, but are inadequate when applied to more complex systems such as the metabolism of a complete cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>An idea of the complexity of the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_network" title="Metabolic network">metabolic networks</a> in cells that contain thousands of different enzymes is given by the figure showing the interactions between just 43 proteins and 40 metabolites to the right: the sequences of genomes provide lists containing anything up to 26.500 genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> However, it is now possible to use this genomic data to reconstruct complete networks of biochemical reactions and produce more <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a> mathematical models that may explain and predict their behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> These models are especially powerful when used to integrate the pathway and metabolite data obtained through classical methods with data on <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> from <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">proteomic</a> and <a href="/wiki/DNA_microarray" title="DNA microarray">DNA microarray</a> studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Using these techniques, a model of human metabolism has now been produced, which will guide future drug discovery and biochemical research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> These models are now used in <a href="/wiki/Network_theory" title="Network theory">network analysis</a>, to classify human diseases into groups that share common proteins or metabolites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacterial metabolic networks are a striking example of <a href="/wiki/Bow_tie_(biology)" title="Bow tie (biology)">bow-tie</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15331224_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15331224-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID12874056_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID12874056-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID16916470_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID16916470-140">[140]</a></sup> organization, an architecture able to input a wide range of nutrients and produce a large variety of products and complex macromolecules using a relatively few intermediate common currencies.
</p><p>A major technological application of this information is <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_engineering" title="Metabolic engineering">metabolic engineering</a>. Here, organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>, plants or <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> are genetically modified to make them more useful in <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and aid the production of <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drugs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> or industrial chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/1,3-Propanediol" title="1,3-Propanediol">1,3-propanediol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shikimic_acid" title="Shikimic acid">shikimic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> These genetic modifications usually aim to reduce the amount of energy used to produce the product, increase yields and reduce the production of wastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_biochemistry" title="History of biochemistry">History of biochemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_molecular_biology" title="History of molecular biology">History of molecular biology</a></div>
<p>The term <i>metabolism</i> is derived from <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> "métabolisme" or <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> μεταβολή – "Metabole" for "a change" which derived from μεταβάλλ –"Metaballein" means "To change"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png/310px-Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png/465px-Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png/620px-Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">Aristotle's metabolism</a> as an open flow model</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_philosophy">Greek philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Greek philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Parts_of_Animals" title="The Parts of Animals">The Parts of Animals</a></i> sets out enough details of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">his views on metabolism</a> for an open flow model to be made. He believed that at each stage of the process, materials from food were transformed, with heat being released as the <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical element</a> of fire, and residual materials being excreted as urine, bile, or faeces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a> described metabolism in his 1260 AD work titled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Risalah_al-Kamiliyyah_fil_Siera_al-Nabawiyyah" title="Al-Risalah al-Kamiliyyah fil Siera al-Nabawiyyah">Al-Risalah al-Kamiliyyah fil Siera al-Nabawiyyah</a> (The Treatise of Kamil on the Prophet's Biography) which included the following phrase "Both the body and its parts are in a continuous state of dissolution and nourishment, so they are inevitably undergoing permanent change."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Application_of_the_scientific_method_and_Modern_metabolic_theories">Application of the scientific method and Modern metabolic theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Application of the scientific method and Modern metabolic theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The history of the scientific study of metabolism spans several centuries and has moved from examining whole animals in early studies, to examining individual metabolic reactions in modern biochemistry.  The first controlled <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a> in human metabolism were published by <a href="/wiki/Santorio_Santorio" title="Santorio Santorio">Santorio Santorio</a> in 1614 in his book <i>Ars de statica medicina</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> He described how he weighed himself before and after eating, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleeping" title="Sleeping">sleep</a>, working, sex, fasting, drinking, and excreting. He found that most of the food he took in was lost through what he called "<a href="/wiki/Insensible_perspiration" title="Insensible perspiration">insensible perspiration</a>".
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SantoriosMeal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="310" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/SantoriosMeal.jpg/150px-SantoriosMeal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/SantoriosMeal.jpg/225px-SantoriosMeal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/SantoriosMeal.jpg/300px-SantoriosMeal.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SantoriosMeal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Santorio_Santorio" title="Santorio Santorio">Santorio Santorio</a> in his steelyard balance, from <i>Ars de statica medicina</i>, first published 1614</div></div></div>
<p>In these early studies, the mechanisms of these metabolic processes had not been identified and a <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vital force</a> was thought to animate living tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> In the 19th century, when studying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> of sugar to <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> concluded that fermentation was catalyzed by substances within the yeast cells he called "ferments". He wrote that "alcoholic fermentation is an act correlated with the life and organization of the yeast cells, not with the death or putrefaction of the cells."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> This discovery, along with the publication by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedrich_Woehler" title="Friedrich Woehler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> in 1828 of a paper on the chemical synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> and is notable for being the first organic compound prepared from wholly inorganic precursors. This proved that the organic compounds and chemical reactions found in cells were no different in principle than any other part of chemistry.
</p><p>It was the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> at the beginning of the 20th century by <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Buchner" title="Eduard Buchner">Eduard Buchner</a> that separated the study of the chemical reactions of metabolism from the biological study of cells, and marked the beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> The mass of biochemical knowledge grew rapidly throughout the early 20th century. One of the most prolific of these modern biochemists was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hans_Adolf_Krebs" title="Hans Adolf Krebs">Hans Krebs</a> who made huge contributions to the study of metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> He discovered the urea cycle and later, working with <a href="/wiki/Hans_Kornberg" title="Hans Kornberg">Hans Kornberg</a>, the citric acid cycle and the glyoxylate cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kornberg_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kornberg-74">[74]</a></sup> Modern biochemical research has been greatly aided by the development of new techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatography</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMR_spectroscopy" title="NMR spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotopic_labelling" title="Radioisotopic labelling">radioisotopic labelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscopy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a> simulations. These techniques have allowed the discovery and detailed analysis of the many molecules and metabolic pathways in cells.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFKrebsJohnson1937">Krebs HA, Johnson WA (April 1937). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1266984" rel="nofollow">"Metabolism of ketonic acids in animal tissues"</a>. <i>The Biochemical Journal</i>. <b>31</b> (4): 645–60. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1042%2Fbj0310645" rel="nofollow">10.1042/bj0310645</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1266984" rel="nofollow">1266984</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16746382" rel="nofollow">16746382</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Biochemical+Journal&amp;rft.atitle=Metabolism+of+ketonic+acids+in+animal+tissues&amp;rft.volume=31&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=645-60&amp;rft.date=1937-04&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC1266984%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F16746382&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1042%2Fbj0310645&amp;rft.aulast=Krebs&amp;rft.aufirst=HA&amp;rft.au=Johnson%2C+WA&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC1266984&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMetabolism"></span></span>
